## Chapter: Chapter 3: Repeated Games:

### Introduction

In the realm of microeconomic theory, the concept of repeated games holds a significant place. This chapter, "Repeated Games," delves into the intricacies of these games, providing a comprehensive understanding of their dynamics and implications. 

Repeated games are a type of dynamic game where the same game, or stage game, is played over multiple periods. The strategic aspect of these games lies in the fact that players remember past actions and can use this information to strategize for future rounds. This memory of past actions introduces a layer of complexity and strategic depth to the game, making it a rich area of study in game theory and mechanism design.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental concepts of repeated games, including the notions of reputation, credibility, and commitment. We will also delve into the various solution concepts for repeated games, such as the subgame perfect equilibrium and the folk theorem. These concepts will be illustrated with a variety of examples and case studies, providing a practical understanding of the theory.

We will also discuss the role of repeated games in various economic scenarios, such as bargaining, contract theory, and auctions. By the end of this chapter, readers should have a solid understanding of the theory of repeated games and its applications in microeconomics.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive guide to repeated games, equipping readers with the necessary tools to analyze and understand these complex games. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a practitioner in the field of microeconomics, this chapter will serve as a valuable resource in your exploration of repeated games.




#### 2.3a Definition of Markov Perfect Equilibrium

Markov perfect equilibrium is a solution concept in game theory that is used to analyze dynamic games. It is a refinement of the Nash equilibrium concept, and it is particularly useful in situations where players' decisions depend on the previous decisions of other players.

In a dynamic game, players make decisions at different points in time, and these decisions can affect the future decisions of other players. The Markov perfect equilibrium concept takes into account this dynamic nature of the game and provides a solution that is robust to changes in the game over time.

The Markov perfect equilibrium is defined as a strategy profile where no player can improve their payoff by unilaterally deviating from their strategy. In other words, each player's strategy is a best response to the strategies of the other players. This concept is named after the Russian mathematician Andrey Kolmogorov, who developed the Markov chain, a mathematical model that is used to describe the evolution of a system over time.

The Markov perfect equilibrium can be illustrated using the example of the airfare game. In this game, two airlines are competing on the same route. The airlines have made sunk investments into their equipment, personnel, and legal framework, and they are engaged in a strategic game when setting prices.

Each airline has a strategy for setting the ticket price for a certain route. At every price-setting opportunity, the airline sets a price that is a best response to the price set by the other airline. This strategy satisfies the Markov perfect equilibrium concept because it does not depend on a history of past observations, and it satisfies the "Markov reaction function" definition because it does not depend on other information which is irrelevant to revenues and profits.

If both airlines follow this strategy, it forms a Markov perfect equilibrium. This means that each airline is playing a best response to the other airline's strategy, and there is no higher-payoff alternative strategy for itself. This equilibrium is robust to changes in the game over time, as each airline's strategy is a best response to the strategies of the other players.

In the next section, we will explore the properties of Markov perfect equilibrium and discuss its applications in various games.

#### 2.3b Properties of Markov Perfect Equilibrium

The Markov perfect equilibrium has several important properties that make it a useful solution concept in game theory. These properties are discussed below:

1. **Robustness:** As mentioned earlier, the Markov perfect equilibrium is robust to changes in the game over time. This means that if the game dynamics change, the Markov perfect equilibrium remains a solution. This property is particularly useful in dynamic games where the game environment can change rapidly.

2. **Subgame perfect:** Every Markov perfect equilibrium is also a subgame perfect equilibrium. This means that the strategies of the players are optimal not only in the current game but also in all subgames of the game. This property ensures that the strategies of the players are consistent and coherent throughout the game.

3. **Existence:** The Markov perfect equilibrium always exists in a finite game. This means that there always exists a strategy profile where no player can improve their payoff by unilaterally deviating from their strategy. This property is particularly useful in situations where the game has a large number of players and strategies.

4. **Uniqueness:** In many games, the Markov perfect equilibrium is unique. This means that there is only one strategy profile that satisfies the Markov perfect equilibrium concept. This property simplifies the analysis of the game and allows for clear predictions about the behavior of the players.

5. **Computability:** The Markov perfect equilibrium can be computed using various algorithms. This means that it is possible to find the Markov perfect equilibrium in a systematic and efficient manner. This property is particularly useful in situations where the game has a large number of players and strategies, and finding the equilibrium requires the use of computational methods.

In the next section, we will discuss some examples of games where the Markov perfect equilibrium can be applied.

#### 2.3c Applications in Game Theory

The Markov perfect equilibrium concept has been widely applied in various fields of game theory. In this section, we will discuss some of these applications.

1. **Dynamic Contracts:** The Markov perfect equilibrium has been used to analyze dynamic contracts in various settings. For example, in the context of a principal-agent relationship, the principal and the agent can use the Markov perfect equilibrium to design a contract that is robust to changes in the environment (Hart and Holmström, 1987).

2. **Repeated Games:** In repeated games, the players interact with each other multiple times. The Markov perfect equilibrium can be used to analyze the behavior of the players in these games. For example, in the context of a prisoner's dilemma, the Markov perfect equilibrium can be used to determine the optimal strategies for the players (Axelrod, 1984).

3. **Market Equilibrium:** The Markov perfect equilibrium has been used to analyze market equilibrium in various settings. For example, in the context of a Cournot competition, the Markov perfect equilibrium can be used to determine the optimal prices for the firms (Hart and Tirole, 1994).

4. **Computational Economics:** The Markov perfect equilibrium has been used in computational economics to analyze the behavior of economic agents in dynamic environments. For example, in the context of agent-based computational economics, the Markov perfect equilibrium can be used to design agents that are robust to changes in the environment (Epstein and Axtell, 1996).

5. **Biological Evolution:** The Markov perfect equilibrium has been used to analyze biological evolution in various settings. For example, in the context of evolutionary game theory, the Markov perfect equilibrium can be used to determine the optimal strategies for the players (Nowak and Sigmund, 1993).

In conclusion, the Markov perfect equilibrium is a powerful solution concept that has been widely applied in various fields of game theory. Its properties make it a useful tool for analyzing dynamic games and understanding the behavior of economic agents in dynamic environments.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of dynamic games, a critical component of advanced game theory and mechanism design. We have explored the intricacies of these games, understanding how they differ from static games and the implications of these differences. We have also examined the various strategies and payoffs associated with dynamic games, and how these can be used to model real-world scenarios.

We have learned that dynamic games are characterized by the fact that players' decisions are made over time, and that these decisions can be influenced by the decisions of other players. This adds a layer of complexity to the game, requiring players to not only consider their own decisions, but also the decisions of others and how these decisions might change over time.

We have also seen how dynamic games can be used to model a wide range of real-world scenarios, from pricing strategies in markets to negotiations between parties. By understanding the dynamics of these games, we can gain valuable insights into these scenarios and make more informed decisions.

In conclusion, dynamic games are a powerful tool in the field of game theory and mechanism design. They allow us to model complex real-world scenarios and understand the strategic interactions between players. By studying these games, we can gain a deeper understanding of the strategic decisions made by individuals and organizations, and use this understanding to make more informed decisions in the future.

## Chapter: Chapter 3: Repeated Games:

### Introduction

In the realm of game theory, repeated games hold a unique position. They are a class of games where the same game, or stage game, is played over multiple periods. The strategic aspect of these games lies in the fact that players remember past actions and can use this information to influence future play. This chapter, "Repeated Games," will delve into the intricacies of these games, exploring their structure, strategies, and the implications of different types of repeated games.

Repeated games are a fundamental concept in game theory, providing a framework for understanding strategic interactions that occur over time. They are particularly relevant in situations where players interact repeatedly, such as in business negotiations, political alliances, and social relationships. The key to success in these situations often lies in understanding the dynamics of repeated games and how to strategically use information about past actions.

In this chapter, we will explore the different types of repeated games, including infinitely repeated games, finitely repeated games, and discounted repeated games. We will also discuss the concept of a repeated game equilibrium, which is a state where no player can improve their payoff by unilaterally deviating from their strategy. 

We will also delve into the strategies that players can use in repeated games, such as tit-for-tat strategies, where players retaliate against each other for deviations from a cooperative strategy. We will also discuss the concept of reputation in repeated games, where players can build a reputation for being cooperative or uncooperative, which can influence future play.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of repeated games and their strategic implications. You will be equipped with the tools to analyze and strategize in a variety of repeated game scenarios, whether in business, politics, or social interactions.




#### 2.3b Examples of Markov Perfect Equilibrium

In the previous section, we introduced the concept of Markov perfect equilibrium and illustrated it using the airfare game. In this section, we will explore some more examples of Markov perfect equilibrium in different scenarios.

##### Example 1: The Repeated Prisoner's Dilemma

The repeated prisoner's dilemma is a classic game theory scenario where two prisoners are interrogated separately and each has the option to cooperate with the other by remaining silent, or to defect by betraying the other. The payoff matrix for this game is as follows:

| Prisoner 1's Payoff | Prisoner 2's Payoff |
|--------------------|--------------------|
| Cooperate         | Cooperate        | 3, 3 |
| Cooperate         | Defect          | 0, 5 |
| Defect           | Cooperate        | 5, 0 |
| Defect           | Defect          | 1, 1 |

In this game, the Markov perfect equilibrium is for both prisoners to cooperate. This is because cooperating is a best response to cooperating, and defecting is a best response to defecting. Therefore, both prisoners have a strategy that satisfies the Markov perfect equilibrium concept.

##### Example 2: The Rock-Paper-Scissors Game

The rock-paper-scissors game is a simple game where two players simultaneously choose one of three options: rock, paper, or scissors. Rock beats scissors, scissors beats paper, and paper beats rock. The payoff matrix for this game is as follows:

| Player 1's Payoff | Player 2's Payoff |
|--------------------|--------------------|
| Rock             | Rock             | 0, 0 |
| Rock             | Paper           | -1, 1 |
| Rock             | Scissors        | 1, -1 |
| Paper            | Rock             | 1, -1 |
| Paper            | Paper           | 0, 0 |
| Paper            | Scissors        | -1, 1 |
| Scissors         | Rock             | -1, 1 |
| Scissors         | Paper           | 1, -1 |
| Scissors         | Scissors        | 0, 0 |

In this game, the Markov perfect equilibrium is for both players to choose rock. This is because rock is a best response to rock, and paper and scissors are best responses to paper and scissors, respectively. Therefore, both players have a strategy that satisfies the Markov perfect equilibrium concept.

##### Example 3: The Traffic Game

The traffic game is a dynamic game where two cars are driving on a single-lane road. Each car has the option to overtake the other car or to stay behind. The payoff matrix for this game is as follows:

| Car 1's Payoff | Car 2's Payoff |
|--------------------|--------------------|
| Overtake        | Overtake        | 2, 2 |
| Overtake        | Stay behind     | 1, 3 |
| Stay behind     | Overtake        | 3, 1 |
| Stay behind     | Stay behind     | 0, 0 |

In this game, the Markov perfect equilibrium is for both cars to overtake each other. This is because overtaking is a best response to overtaking, and staying behind is a best response to staying behind. Therefore, both cars have a strategy that satisfies the Markov perfect equilibrium concept.

These examples illustrate the concept of Markov perfect equilibrium in different scenarios. In each case, the Markov perfect equilibrium is a strategy profile where no player can improve their payoff by unilaterally deviating from their strategy. This concept is particularly useful in dynamic games where players' decisions depend on the previous decisions of other players.

#### 2.3c Applications of Markov Perfect Equilibrium

In this section, we will explore some applications of Markov perfect equilibrium in various scenarios. The Markov perfect equilibrium concept is a powerful tool that can be used to analyze a wide range of dynamic games.

##### Example 4: The Cournot Competition

The Cournot competition is a classic game theory scenario where a number of firms simultaneously choose the quantity of a homogeneous product to produce. The price of the product is determined by the total quantity produced. The payoff matrix for this game is as follows:

| Firm's Payoff | Total Quantity |
|--------------------|--------------------|
| Quantity       | Price           |
|--------------------|--------------------|
| q1             | p(q1 + ... + qn) |
|--------------------|--------------------|
| q2             | p(q1 + ... + qn) |
|--------------------|--------------------|
| ...           | ...            |
|--------------------|--------------------|
| qn             | p(q1 + ... + qn) |

In this game, the Markov perfect equilibrium is for all firms to choose the same quantity. This is because choosing the same quantity is a best response to choosing the same quantity, and choosing a different quantity is a best response to choosing a different quantity. Therefore, all firms have a strategy that satisfies the Markov perfect equilibrium concept.

##### Example 5: The Rock-Paper-Scissors-Lizard-Spock Game

The rock-paper-scissors-lizard-spock game is a variation of the rock-paper-scissors game. In this game, players simultaneously choose one of five options: rock, paper, scissors, lizard, or spock. Rock beats scissors, scissors beats paper, paper beats rock, lizard beats spock, spock beats lizard, and lizard beats paper. The payoff matrix for this game is as follows:

| Player 1's Payoff | Player 2's Payoff |
|--------------------|--------------------|
| Rock             | Rock             | 0, 0 |
| Rock             | Paper           | -1, 1 |
| Rock             | Scissors        | 1, -1 |
| Rock             | Lizard          | 2, -2 |
| Rock             | Spock           | -2, 2 |
| Paper            | Rock             | 1, -1 |
| Paper            | Paper           | 0, 0 |
| Paper            | Scissors        | -1, 1 |
| Paper            | Lizard          | 3, -3 |
| Paper            | Spock           | -3, 3 |
| Scissors         | Rock             | -1, 1 |
| Scissors         | Paper           | 1, -1 |
| Scissors         | Scissors        | 0, 0 |
| Scissors         | Lizard          | 2, -2 |
| Scissors         | Spock           | -2, 2 |
| Lizard            | Rock             | -2, 2 |
| Lizard            | Paper           | 3, -3 |
| Lizard            | Scissors        | 2, -2 |
| Lizard            | Lizard          | 0, 0 |
| Lizard            | Spock           | -3, 3 |
| Spock             | Rock             | 2, -2 |
| Spock             | Paper           | -3, 3 |
| Spock             | Scissors        | -2, 2 |
| Spock             | Lizard          | 3, -3 |
| Spock             | Spock           | 0, 0 |

In this game, the Markov perfect equilibrium is for all players to choose the same option. This is because choosing the same option is a best response to choosing the same option, and choosing a different option is a best response to choosing a different option. Therefore, all players have a strategy that satisfies the Markov perfect equilibrium concept.

These examples illustrate the power of the Markov perfect equilibrium concept in analyzing a wide range of dynamic games. By understanding the strategies that satisfy the Markov perfect equilibrium concept, we can gain insights into the behavior of players in these games.




#### 2.3c Application in Game Theory

In the previous sections, we have explored the concept of Markov perfect equilibrium and provided some examples to illustrate its application. In this section, we will delve deeper into the application of Markov perfect equilibrium in game theory.

##### Application in Dynamic Games

Markov perfect equilibrium is particularly useful in dynamic games, where players make decisions sequentially and the outcome of the game depends on the sequence of decisions. In these games, the Markov perfect equilibrium concept helps us identify the strategies that are optimal for each player, given the strategies of the other players.

For instance, in the repeated prisoner's dilemma, the Markov perfect equilibrium strategy for each player is to cooperate, given that the other player is also cooperating. This strategy is optimal because it maximizes the payoff for each player, given the assumption that the other player is also cooperating.

##### Application in Mechanism Design

Markov perfect equilibrium also finds application in mechanism design, which is the process of designing rules or mechanisms to achieve a desired outcome in a strategic environment. In mechanism design, the Markov perfect equilibrium concept helps us identify the strategies that are optimal for each player, given the rules of the mechanism.

For example, in the airfare game, the Markov perfect equilibrium strategy for each airline is to set a price that is equal to the marginal cost, given that the other airlines are also setting a price equal to the marginal cost. This strategy is optimal because it maximizes the profit for each airline, given the assumption that the other airlines are also setting a price equal to the marginal cost.

##### Application in Other Areas of Economics

The concept of Markov perfect equilibrium is not limited to game theory and mechanism design. It has applications in other areas of economics, such as industrial organization, labor economics, and macroeconomics. In these areas, the Markov perfect equilibrium concept helps us understand how rational agents make decisions in strategic environments.

For instance, in industrial organization, the Markov perfect equilibrium concept can be used to analyze pricing strategies in oligopolistic markets. In labor economics, it can be used to understand how workers choose between different jobs. In macroeconomics, it can be used to analyze the behavior of economic agents in dynamic markets.

In conclusion, the concept of Markov perfect equilibrium is a powerful tool in game theory and mechanism design. It helps us understand how rational agents make decisions in strategic environments, and it has applications in a wide range of areas in economics.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of dynamic games, a critical component of advanced game theory and mechanism design. We have explored the intricacies of these games, understanding how they differ from static games and the implications of these differences. We have also examined the various strategies and outcomes that can arise in dynamic games, and how these can be influenced by factors such as timing and information.

We have also discussed the importance of understanding the dynamics of these games in order to make informed decisions and predictions. The concepts of Markov perfect equilibrium and subgame perfect equilibrium have been introduced, providing a framework for analyzing these games and predicting their outcomes.

In conclusion, dynamic games are a complex and fascinating area of study, with wide-ranging implications for various fields such as economics, political science, and biology. By understanding the principles and strategies of these games, we can gain valuable insights into the behavior of rational agents in dynamic environments.

## Chapter: Chapter 4: Mechanism Design

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 4 of "Microeconomic Theory: A Comprehensive Guide". This chapter is dedicated to the fascinating field of mechanism design, a critical component of microeconomic theory. Mechanism design is a branch of game theory that deals with the design of mechanisms or rules to achieve a desired outcome in a strategic environment. It is a powerful tool that can be used to solve a wide range of problems in economics, politics, and other fields.

In this chapter, we will delve into the fundamental concepts of mechanism design, starting with the basic principles and gradually moving on to more complex topics. We will explore the different types of mechanisms, their properties, and how they can be used to solve various economic problems. We will also discuss the role of incentives in mechanism design and how they can be used to align the interests of different agents.

We will also delve into the famous "revelation principle" in mechanism design, which provides a powerful tool for designing mechanisms. The revelation principle states that any mechanism that is incentive compatible can be simplified to a direct mechanism. This principle has profound implications for the design of mechanisms and has been used to solve many important problems in economics.

Finally, we will discuss some of the key applications of mechanism design in economics, such as the design of auctions, voting systems, and matching mechanisms. We will also touch upon some of the challenges and limitations of mechanism design, and how they can be addressed.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive introduction to mechanism design, suitable for both beginners and advanced students of microeconomic theory. We will use a clear and accessible style, with many examples and exercises to help you understand the concepts and apply them to real-world problems.

So, let's embark on this exciting journey into the world of mechanism design.




#### 2.4a Definition of Sequential Equilibrium

Sequential equilibrium is a refinement of Nash equilibrium for extensive form games, introduced by David M. Kreps and Robert Wilson. It is a concept that extends the idea of a perfect Bayesian equilibrium, providing a more nuanced understanding of strategic decision-making in dynamic games.

A sequential equilibrium is a profile of strategies and beliefs that satisfies certain conditions. Informally, an assessment is a perfect Bayesian equilibrium if its strategies are sensible given its beliefs, and its beliefs are confirmed on the outcome path given by its strategies. The definition of sequential equilibrium further requires that there be arbitrarily small perturbations of beliefs and associated strategies with the same property.

The formal definition of a strategy being sensible given a belief is straightforward. The strategy should simply maximize expected payoff in every information set. For those information sets that are reached with positive probability given the strategies, the beliefs should be the conditional probability distribution on the nodes of the information set, given that it is reached. This entails the application of Bayes' rule.

However, it is far from straightforward to define what a sensible belief should be for those information sets that are reached with probability zero, given the strategies. This is the main conceptual contribution of Kreps and Wilson. Their consistency requirement is the following: The assessment should be a limit point of a sequence of totally mixed strategy profiles and associated sensible beliefs, in the above straightforward sense.

Sequential equilibrium is a further refinement of subgame perfect equilibrium and even perfect Bayesian equilibrium. It is itself refined by other equilibrium concepts, such as subgame perfect equilibrium and perfect Bayesian equilibrium. However, it provides a more detailed understanding of strategic decision-making in dynamic games, making it a valuable tool for analyzing a wide range of economic phenomena.

#### 2.4b Properties of Sequential Equilibrium

Sequential equilibrium, as a refinement of Nash equilibrium, possesses several key properties that make it a powerful tool for analyzing dynamic games. These properties are derived from the fundamental principles of rationality and consistency, and they provide a deeper understanding of strategic decision-making in dynamic environments.

##### Consistency

The first property of sequential equilibrium is consistency. This property ensures that the beliefs of the players are consistent with their strategies. In other words, the strategies of the players should be such that they maximize their expected payoff, given their beliefs. This property is crucial in ensuring that the assessment is a perfect Bayesian equilibrium.

##### Sensitivity to Perturbations

The second property of sequential equilibrium is sensitivity to perturbations. This property requires that there be arbitrarily small perturbations of beliefs and associated strategies with the same property. This means that the assessment should be robust to small changes in the beliefs and strategies of the players. This property is important in ensuring that the assessment is not overly sensitive to small changes in the game.

##### Subgame Perfect Equilibrium

The third property of sequential equilibrium is that it is a subgame perfect equilibrium. This means that the assessment is a perfect Bayesian equilibrium for every subgame of the original game. This property is important in ensuring that the assessment is not overly sensitive to the specific details of the game.

##### Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium

The fourth property of sequential equilibrium is that it is a perfect Bayesian equilibrium. This means that the assessment is a limit point of a sequence of totally mixed strategy profiles and associated sensible beliefs. This property is crucial in ensuring that the assessment is a stable equilibrium.

##### Further Refinements

Finally, sequential equilibrium is itself refined by other equilibrium concepts, such as subgame perfect equilibrium and perfect Bayesian equilibrium. This means that sequential equilibrium is a further refinement of these concepts, providing a more detailed understanding of strategic decision-making in dynamic games.

In conclusion, the properties of sequential equilibrium make it a powerful tool for analyzing dynamic games. By ensuring consistency, sensitivity to perturbations, subgame perfect equilibrium, perfect Bayesian equilibrium, and further refinements, sequential equilibrium provides a deeper understanding of strategic decision-making in dynamic environments.

#### 2.4c Applications in Game Theory

Sequential equilibrium has been applied to a wide range of games in economics, political science, and other fields. In this section, we will explore some of these applications, focusing on the use of sequential equilibrium in the study of auctions, bargaining, and voting systems.

##### Auctions

In the context of auctions, sequential equilibrium has been used to analyze the behavior of bidders in auctions with multiple rounds. For example, in a second-price sealed-bid auction, bidders submit their bids simultaneously and the highest bidder wins the auction, paying the second-highest bid. In this game, sequential equilibrium can be used to determine the optimal bidding strategies for the bidders, taking into account their beliefs about the other bidders' valuations and bidding strategies.

##### Bargaining

Sequential equilibrium has also been applied to the study of bargaining games. In these games, two or more players negotiate over the division of a pie, with the goal of reaching an agreement that maximizes their combined payoff. Sequential equilibrium can be used to analyze the bargaining process, determining the optimal strategies for the players and the conditions under which an agreement can be reached.

##### Voting Systems

In the field of political science, sequential equilibrium has been used to study voting systems. For example, in a voting system with multiple candidates and voters who have different preferences over the candidates, sequential equilibrium can be used to determine the optimal voting strategies for the voters, taking into account their beliefs about the preferences of the other voters.

##### Further Applications

These are just a few examples of the many applications of sequential equilibrium in game theory. Sequential equilibrium has also been used to study other types of games, such as contract bridge, the game of chess, and the market for lemons. It has also been applied to a variety of economic phenomena, including the pricing of options, the behavior of firms in oligopolistic markets, and the dynamics of asset prices.

In conclusion, sequential equilibrium is a powerful tool for analyzing dynamic games. Its properties of consistency, sensitivity to perturbations, subgame perfect equilibrium, perfect Bayesian equilibrium, and further refinements make it a valuable tool for understanding strategic decision-making in a wide range of contexts.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of dynamic games, a crucial aspect of microeconomic theory. We have explored the fundamental concepts, strategies, and outcomes of dynamic games, and how they differ from static games. We have also examined the role of time in these games, and how it can influence the behavior of players and the overall outcome of the game.

We have learned that dynamic games are characterized by the fact that players make decisions sequentially, and that these decisions can be influenced by the decisions of previous players. This sequential nature of decision-making adds a layer of complexity to the game, and requires players to consider not only their current decisions, but also the potential future decisions of their opponents.

We have also seen how dynamic games can be modeled using various mathematical tools, such as the Bellman equation and the concept of a Nash equilibrium. These tools allow us to analyze the strategies and outcomes of dynamic games in a systematic and rigorous manner.

In conclusion, dynamic games are a rich and complex field of study, with many interesting and important applications in economics and other disciplines. By understanding the principles and techniques of dynamic games, we can gain a deeper understanding of the strategic interactions that occur in many areas of life.

### Exercises

#### Exercise 1
Consider a dynamic game with two players, A and B. Player A moves first, and then player B. Each player has two strategies, cooperate (C) or defect (D). The payoff matrix for player A is as follows:

| Player A's Strategies | Player B's Strategies |
|---------------------|---------------------|
| Cooperate (C)      | Cooperate (C)     | 3, 3 |
| Cooperate (C)      | Defect (D)       | 0, 5 |
| Defect (D)        | Cooperate (C)     | 5, 0 |
| Defect (D)        | Defect (D)       | 1, 1 |

a) What is the Nash equilibrium of this game?
b) What is the optimal strategy for player A, assuming that player B will always cooperate?
c) What is the optimal strategy for player A, assuming that player B will always defect?

#### Exercise 2
Consider a dynamic game with three players, A, B, and C. Players A and B move simultaneously, and then player C moves. Each player has two strategies, cooperate (C) or defect (D). The payoff matrix for player A is as follows:

| Player A's Strategies | Player B's Strategies | Player C's Strategies |
|---------------------|---------------------|---------------------|
| Cooperate (C)      | Cooperate (C)     | Cooperate (C)     | 4, 4, 4 |
| Cooperate (C)      | Cooperate (C)     | Defect (D)       | 1, 1, 3 |
| Cooperate (C)      | Defect (D)       | Cooperate (C)     | 3, 1, 1 |
| Cooperate (C)      | Defect (D)       | Defect (D)       | 0, 5, 5 |
| Defect (D)        | Cooperate (C)     | Cooperate (C)     | 4, 4, 4 |
| Defect (D)        | Cooperate (C)     | Defect (D)       | 1, 1, 3 |
| Defect (D)        | Defect (D)       | Cooperate (C)     | 3, 1, 1 |
| Defect (D)        | Defect (D)       | Defect (D)       | 0, 5, 5 |

a) What is the Nash equilibrium of this game?
b) What is the optimal strategy for player A, assuming that players B and C will always cooperate?
c) What is the optimal strategy for player A, assuming that players B and C will always defect?

| Defect (D)        | Punish (P)       | -2, 8 |
| Punish (P)        | Cooperate (C)     | 7, -1 |
| Punish (P)        | Defect (D)       | 8, -2 |
| Punish (P)        | Punish (P)       | 9, -3 |

a) What is the Nash equilibrium of this game?
b) What is the optimal strategy for player A, assuming that player B will always cooperate?
c) What is the optimal strategy for player A, assuming that player B will always defect?

b) What is the optimal strategy for player A, assuming that player B will always cooperate?
c) What is the optimal strategy for player A, assuming that player B will always defect?

## Chapter: Chapter 3: Repeated Games

### Introduction

In the realm of game theory, repeated games hold a unique position. Unlike one-shot games, where players make decisions based on their own preferences and the game structure, repeated games allow for a dynamic interplay between players. This chapter, "Repeated Games," will delve into the fascinating world of these games, exploring their strategic complexities and the insights they offer into human behavior.

Repeated games are characterized by the fact that players interact with each other multiple times, often in a sequential manner. This repeated interaction can lead to a variety of interesting phenomena, such as the possibility of cooperation, the emergence of patterns, and the influence of reputation. These phenomena are not only of theoretical interest, but also have important implications for a wide range of real-world scenarios, from negotiations and contracts to international relations and evolutionary biology.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental concepts of repeated games, including the notions of a repeated game, a strategy, and a payoff. We will also discuss various solution concepts, such as the Nash equilibrium and the subgame perfect equilibrium, and how they apply to repeated games. Furthermore, we will examine some of the most influential models of repeated games, such as the infinitely repeated game and the discounted infinitely repeated game.

Throughout the chapter, we will use mathematical notation to formalize these concepts and models. For example, we might denote the payoff of player $i$ in a repeated game as $u_i$, and the strategy of player $i$ as $s_i$. We will also use the powerful language of game theory to describe and analyze these games, employing concepts such as best response, dominant strategy, and mixed strategy.

By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of the principles and techniques of repeated games, and be able to apply them to a variety of real-world scenarios. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or simply a curious reader, we hope that this chapter will provide you with a deeper understanding of the strategic dynamics of repeated games.




#### 2.4b Examples of Sequential Equilibrium

In this section, we will explore some examples of sequential equilibrium to further illustrate the concept.

##### Example 1: The Rock-Paper-Scissors Game

Consider the classic game of Rock-Paper-Scissors. In this game, two players simultaneously choose one of three options: rock, paper, or scissors. Rock beats scissors, scissors beats paper, and paper beats rock. 

In the context of sequential equilibrium, we can consider the strategies of the players and their beliefs about the other player's strategies. For instance, if Player 1 believes that Player 2 will always choose rock, then Player 1's best strategy would be to choose paper. However, if Player 1 believes that Player 2 will choose paper with a probability of 0.5 and rock with a probability of 0.5, then Player 1's best strategy would be to choose scissors.

The beliefs of the players can also be influenced by their past actions. For example, if Player 1 has chosen rock in the previous round and Player 2 has chosen paper, then Player 1 might believe that Player 2 will choose scissors in the next round. This belief could influence Player 1's strategy in the next round.

##### Example 2: The Market Equilibrium Computation

In the context of market equilibrium computation, sequential equilibrium can be used to model the strategic interactions between buyers and sellers. For instance, consider a market where buyers and sellers interact sequentially. The buyers' strategies could be to choose the price they are willing to pay, while the sellers' strategies could be to choose the price they are willing to sell at.

The beliefs of the buyers and sellers could be influenced by their past actions and the actions of the other players. For example, if a buyer has chosen to pay a high price in the previous round and a seller has chosen to sell at a high price, then the buyer might believe that the seller will choose to sell at a high price in the next round. This belief could influence the buyer's strategy in the next round.

In conclusion, sequential equilibrium provides a powerful framework for analyzing strategic interactions in dynamic games. By considering the strategies and beliefs of the players, we can gain a deeper understanding of the outcomes of these games.

#### 2.4c Properties of Sequential Equilibrium

Sequential equilibrium, as a refinement of Nash equilibrium, possesses several key properties that make it a valuable tool in the analysis of dynamic games. These properties are derived from the fundamental principles of game theory and provide insights into the strategic interactions between players.

##### Existence

The existence of a sequential equilibrium is guaranteed under certain conditions. For instance, if the game is finite and the players' strategies are finite, then a sequential equilibrium exists. This is a direct consequence of the Kreps-Wilson theorem, which states that every finite game with finite strategies has a sequential equilibrium.

##### Uniqueness

The uniqueness of a sequential equilibrium is a desirable property that is not always guaranteed. In some cases, a game may have multiple sequential equilibria. For example, in the Rock-Paper-Scissors game, if both players believe that the other player will choose each option with a probability of 0.33, then both players' best strategy would be to choose each option with a probability of 0.33. This would result in a sequential equilibrium where each player chooses rock, paper, and scissors with a probability of 0.33.

However, in many cases, a game will have a unique sequential equilibrium. This is often the case when the players' strategies are not symmetric, or when the players' beliefs about the other player's strategies are not uniform.

##### Robustness

The robustness of a sequential equilibrium refers to its stability against small perturbations. A sequential equilibrium is robust if small changes in the players' strategies or beliefs do not lead to a significant change in the equilibrium outcome. This property is important in practice, as it allows us to predict the outcome of a game even when the players' strategies and beliefs are not perfectly known.

##### Subgame Perfection

A sequential equilibrium is subgame perfect if it is also a Nash equilibrium in every subgame of the original game. This property is desirable because it ensures that the players' strategies are not only optimal in the overall game, but also in every smaller game within the overall game. This is particularly important in games with multiple stages, where the players' strategies in earlier stages can affect the outcome of later stages.

In conclusion, the properties of sequential equilibrium make it a powerful tool for analyzing dynamic games. By understanding these properties, we can gain a deeper understanding of the strategic interactions between players and predict the outcomes of games more accurately.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of dynamic games, a critical component of advanced game theory and mechanism design. We have explored the fundamental concepts, strategies, and outcomes of dynamic games, and how they differ from static games. We have also examined the role of time in these games, and how it can influence the behavior of players and the overall outcome.

We have learned that dynamic games are characterized by the fact that players make decisions sequentially, and that these decisions can be influenced by the decisions of previous players. This sequential nature adds a layer of complexity to the game, as players must not only consider their own best interests, but also the potential reactions of their opponents.

We have also seen how dynamic games can be modeled using various mathematical tools, such as the payoff matrix and the Nash equilibrium. These tools allow us to predict the outcome of a dynamic game, and to identify the strategies that are most likely to be chosen by the players.

In conclusion, dynamic games are a rich and complex field of study, with many interesting and important applications. By understanding the principles and techniques of dynamic games, we can gain valuable insights into a wide range of real-world situations, from economic competition to political negotiations.

#### Exercise 1

| Player 1's strategy | Player 2's strategy | Player 1's payoff | Player 2's payoff |
|--------------------|--------------------|-----------------|-----------------|
| A                | A                | 3               | 4               |
| A                | B                | 2               | 5               |
| B                | A                | 4               | 3               |
| B                | B                | 5               | 2               |

b) What is the outcome of the game if both players choose the strategy A?
c) What is the outcome of the game if both players choose the strategy B?

## Chapter: Chapter 3: Repeated Games

### Introduction

In the realm of microeconomic theory, the concept of repeated games holds a significant place. This chapter, "Repeated Games," delves into the intricacies of these games, providing a comprehensive understanding of their dynamics and implications. 

Repeated games are a fundamental concept in game theory, particularly in the context of microeconomics. They are characterized by the fact that the same game, or stage of a game, is played over multiple periods. This repetition introduces a unique set of challenges and opportunities for players, as they must not only consider their immediate decisions but also anticipate the future behavior of their opponents.

In this chapter, we will explore the theoretical underpinnings of repeated games, including the concept of a repeated game, the types of strategies that can be employed, and the conditions under which a strategy can be considered stable. We will also delve into the practical implications of repeated games, discussing how they can be used to model real-world scenarios in economics, politics, and other fields.

We will also examine the concept of reputation in repeated games, a crucial factor that can influence the behavior of players and the outcomes of the game. Reputation, in this context, refers to the perception that players have of each other's behavior over time. It can be a powerful tool in the hands of players, allowing them to influence the behavior of their opponents and potentially alter the course of the game.

Finally, we will discuss the role of learning in repeated games. Learning, in this context, refers to the process by which players adjust their strategies in response to the behavior of their opponents. This process can be crucial in repeated games, as it allows players to adapt to the changing circumstances of the game and potentially gain an advantage over their opponents.

In summary, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of repeated games, equipping readers with the tools and knowledge they need to analyze and understand these complex scenarios. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a practitioner in the field of microeconomics, we hope that this chapter will serve as a valuable resource in your exploration of repeated games.




#### 2.4c Application in Game Theory

In this section, we will explore some applications of sequential equilibrium in game theory. We will focus on the use of sequential equilibrium in the context of market equilibrium computation and online computation.

##### Market Equilibrium Computation

As we have seen in the previous section, sequential equilibrium can be used to model the strategic interactions between buyers and sellers in a market. The concept of sequential equilibrium can be particularly useful in situations where the players' strategies and beliefs are influenced by their past actions and the actions of the other players.

For instance, consider a market where buyers and sellers interact sequentially. The buyers' strategies could be to choose the price they are willing to pay, while the sellers' strategies could be to choose the price they are willing to sell at. The beliefs of the buyers and sellers could be influenced by their past actions and the actions of the other players. For example, if a buyer has chosen to pay a high price in the previous round and a seller has chosen to sell at a high price, then the buyer might believe that the seller will choose to sell at a high price in the next round. This belief could influence the buyer's strategy in the next round.

##### Online Computation

Sequential equilibrium can also be applied in the context of online computation. Recently, Gao, Peysakhovich, and Kroer presented an algorithm for online computation of market equilibrium. This algorithm uses the concept of sequential equilibrium to compute the equilibrium prices and quantities in a market in real-time.

The algorithm works by iteratively updating the prices and quantities in the market based on the strategies and beliefs of the buyers and sellers. The strategies of the players are determined by their past actions and the actions of the other players, while their beliefs are updated based on the current prices and quantities in the market.

In conclusion, sequential equilibrium is a powerful tool in game theory that can be used to model and analyze a wide range of strategic interactions. Its applications in market equilibrium computation and online computation demonstrate its versatility and potential for further research.

The concepts and tools discussed in this chapter are essential for anyone seeking to understand and analyze these games.

### Exercises

#### Exercise 1
Consider a dynamic game where two firms compete in a duopoly market. Firm 1 can choose to invest in a new technology or stick with the current one, while Firm 2 can choose to enter the market or stay out. If both firms invest in the new technology, they will have a competitive advantage and earn higher profits. If only one firm invests, they will have a monopoly and earn even higher profits. If both firms stick with the current technology, they will earn lower profits. If Firm 2 enters the market, both firms will earn lower profits. Model this game and find the Nash equilibrium.

#### Exercise 2
In a dynamic game, three countries are competing to produce and export a certain commodity. Each country can choose to invest in technology to increase production or stick with the current technology. If all three countries invest, they will have a competitive advantage and earn higher profits. If only two countries invest, they will have a duopoly and earn higher profits. If only one country invests, they will have a monopoly and earn even higher profits. If no country invests, they will earn lower profits. Model this game and find the Nash equilibrium.

#### Exercise 3
Consider a dynamic game where two firms are competing in a duopoly market. Firm 1 can choose to invest in a new technology or stick with the current one, while Firm 2 can choose to enter the market or stay out. If both firms invest in the new technology, they will have a competitive advantage and earn higher profits. If only one firm invests, they will have a monopoly and earn even higher profits. If both firms stick with the current technology, they will earn lower profits. If Firm 2 enters the market, both firms will earn lower profits. However, if Firm 2 enters the market and Firm 1 does not invest, Firm 1 will earn lower profits. Model this game and find the Nash equilibrium.

#### Exercise 4
In a dynamic game, two countries are competing to produce and export a certain commodity. Each country can choose to invest in technology to increase production or stick with the current technology. If both countries invest, they will have a competitive advantage and earn higher profits. If only one country invests, they will have a monopoly and earn even higher profits. If no country invests, they will earn lower profits. However, if one country invests and the other does not, the investing country will earn higher profits. Model this game and find the Nash equilibrium.

#### Exercise 5
Consider a dynamic game where two firms are competing in a duopoly market. Firm 1 can choose to invest in a new technology or stick with the current one, while Firm 2 can choose to enter the market or stay out. If both firms invest in the new technology, they will have a competitive advantage and earn higher profits. If only one firm invests, they will have a monopoly and earn even higher profits. If both firms stick with the current technology, they will earn lower profits. If Firm 2 enters the market, both firms will earn lower profits. However, if Firm 2 enters the market and Firm 1 does not invest, Firm 1 will earn lower profits. Additionally, if both firms invest and Firm 2 enters the market, they will earn even higher profits. Model this game and find the Nash equilibrium.

## Chapter: Microeconomic Theory II: A Comprehensive Guide to Advanced Game Theory and Mechanism Design

### Introduction

In the previous chapter, we explored the fundamentals of microeconomic theory, including consumer and producer behavior, market equilibrium, and market failure. In this chapter, we will delve deeper into the world of microeconomics and focus on the topic of mechanism design. Mechanism design is a branch of game theory that deals with the design of mechanisms or rules to achieve a desired outcome in a strategic environment. It is a powerful tool that can be used to solve a wide range of economic problems, from allocating resources efficiently to designing auctions and voting systems.

In this chapter, we will cover various topics related to mechanism design, including the basics of mechanism design, the revelation principle, and the implementation problem. We will also explore different types of mechanisms, such as the Vickrey-Clarke-Groves (VCG) mechanism and the Groves mechanism. Additionally, we will discuss the applications of mechanism design in different fields, such as economics, political science, and computer science.

Overall, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive guide to advanced mechanism design, equipping readers with the necessary knowledge and tools to understand and apply mechanism design in various real-world scenarios. By the end of this chapter, readers will have a deeper understanding of the principles and techniques of mechanism design and be able to apply them to solve complex economic problems. So let us dive into the world of mechanism design and discover its potential in solving real-world economic problems.

In a dynamic game, two countries are competing to produce and export a certain commodity. Each country can choose to invest in technology to increase production or stick with the current technology. If both countries invest, they will have a competitive advantage and earn higher profits. If only one country invests, they will have a monopoly and earn even higher profits. If no country invests, they will earn lower profits. However, if one country invests and the other does not, the investing country will earn higher profits. Model this game and find the Nash equilibrium.

#### Exercise 5
Consider a dynamic game where two firms are competing in a duopoly market. Firm 1 can choose to invest in a new technology or stick with the current one, while Firm 2 can choose to enter the market or stay out. If both firms invest in the new technology, they will have a competitive advantage and earn higher profits. If only one firm invests, they will have a monopoly and earn even higher profits. If both firms stick with the current technology, they will earn lower profits. If Firm 2 enters the market, both firms will earn lower profits. However, if Firm 2 enters the market and Firm 1 does not invest, Firm 1 will earn lower profits. Additionally, if both firms invest and Firm 2 enters the market, they will earn even higher profits. Model this game and find the Nash equilibrium.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the fascinating world of dynamic games, where players make decisions over time and their decisions can affect the future outcomes of the game. We have learned about the different types of dynamic games, including sequential and simultaneous games, and how they can be modeled using game theory. We have also delved into the concept of Nash equilibrium and how it applies to dynamic games.

One of the key takeaways from this chapter is the importance of timing in decision-making. In dynamic games, players must not only consider their current decisions but also anticipate how their decisions will affect future outcomes. This requires a deep understanding of the game dynamics and the ability to think strategically.

Another important concept we have explored is the role of information in dynamic games. In many real-world scenarios, players do not have complete information about the game or their opponents. This adds an extra layer of complexity to the game and requires players to make decisions based on incomplete information.

Overall, this chapter has provided a comprehensive guide to understanding dynamic games and their applications in various fields. By studying dynamic games, we can gain valuable insights into real-world decision-making processes and develop strategies to optimize our outcomes.

## Chapter: Microeconomic Theory II: A Comprehensive Guide to Advanced Game Theory and Mechanism Design
