## Chapter 4: Mechanism Design:




### Introduction

In the previous chapters, we have explored the fundamentals of microeconomic theory, game theory, and mechanism design. We have also delved into static games, where players make decisions simultaneously or sequentially without the ability to observe the actions of other players. However, in many real-world scenarios, decisions are not made in isolation, but rather in a dynamic environment where players interact with each other over time. This is where dynamic games with incomplete information come into play.

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of dynamic games with incomplete information. We will explore how players make decisions in a dynamic setting, where they not only have to consider their current actions but also anticipate the actions of other players in the future. We will also examine how incomplete information can affect the outcome of these games, and how players can strategically use this information to their advantage.

We will begin by discussing the basic concepts of dynamic games, including the role of time and the ability of players to observe the actions of others. We will then move on to more advanced topics, such as the concept of a Nash equilibrium in a dynamic setting, and how it differs from a static Nash equilibrium. We will also explore the role of reputation in dynamic games, and how it can influence the behavior of players.

Finally, we will delve into the realm of mechanism design in dynamic games, where we will examine how mechanisms can be designed to incentivize players to behave in a certain way over time. We will also discuss the challenges and limitations of designing mechanisms in a dynamic setting, and how these can be addressed.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a comprehensive understanding of dynamic games with incomplete information, and be equipped with the tools to analyze and design mechanisms for these types of games. So let's dive in and explore the exciting world of dynamic games!




### Subsection: 4.1a Definition of Bayesian Nash Equilibrium

In the previous chapter, we introduced the concept of a Nash equilibrium, where a strategy profile is a Nash equilibrium if every strategy in that profile is a best response to every other strategy in the profile. However, in many real-world scenarios, players do not have complete information about the game. This is where the concept of Bayesian Nash equilibrium (BNE) comes into play.

A Bayesian Nash equilibrium (BNE) is a strategy profile that maximizes the expected payoff for each player given their beliefs and given the strategies played by the other players. That is, a strategy profile $\sigma$ is a Bayesian Nash equilibrium if and only if for every player $i$, keeping the strategies of every other player fixed, strategy $\sigma_i$ maximizes the expected payoff of player $i$ according to that player's beliefs.

In a Bayesian game, a player's beliefs about the state of nature are formed by conditioning the prior probabilities $p$ on the player's own type according to Bayes' rule. This means that each player's beliefs about the state of nature are influenced by their own type, as well as the types of the other players.

The concept of BNE is particularly useful in dynamic games with incomplete information, where players not only have to consider their current actions but also anticipate the actions of other players in the future. By incorporating beliefs about the state of nature into the definition of a Nash equilibrium, we can better understand how players make decisions in these complex games.

In the next section, we will explore the properties of BNE and how it differs from a traditional Nash equilibrium. We will also discuss the role of beliefs in BNE and how they can affect the outcome of a game.




### Subsection: 4.1b Examples of Bayesian Nash Equilibrium

In this section, we will explore some examples of Bayesian Nash equilibrium (BNE) in dynamic games with incomplete information. These examples will help us understand the concept of BNE and how it differs from a traditional Nash equilibrium.

#### Example 1: The Sheriff's Dilemma

Consider the game of the Sheriff's Dilemma, where a sheriff faces an armed suspect. Both must simultaneously decide whether to shoot the other or not. The suspect can either be of type "criminal" or type "civilian". The sheriff has only one type. The suspect knows its type and the Sheriff's type, but the Sheriff does not know the suspect's type. This is an example of a Bayesian game, as there is incomplete information (because the suspect has private information), making it a Bayesian game.

The payoff matrix of this game for both players depends on the type of the suspect. If both players are rational and both know that both players are rational and everything that is known by any player is known to be known by every player (i.e. player 1 knows player 2 knows that player 1 is rational), then the strategy profile (shoot, shoot) is a Bayesian Nash equilibrium. This is because if the suspect is a criminal, both players are better off shooting, and if the suspect is a civilian, both players are better off not shooting.

#### Example 2: Market Equilibrium Computation

Consider a market equilibrium computation game, where a set of buyers and sellers interact in a market. Each buyer has a valuation for a good, and each seller has a cost of producing the good. The buyers and sellers simultaneously decide whether to participate in the market or not. This is an example of a Bayesian game, as there is incomplete information (because the buyers and sellers have private information about their valuations and costs), making it a Bayesian game.

The payoff matrix of this game for both players depends on the type of the buyers and sellers. If both players are rational and both know that both players are rational and everything that is known by any player is known to be known by every player (i.e. player 1 knows player 2 knows that player 1 is rational), then the strategy profile (participate, participate) is a Bayesian Nash equilibrium. This is because if the buyers and sellers are rational, they will all participate in the market, leading to a market equilibrium.

These examples illustrate the concept of Bayesian Nash equilibrium and how it can be applied in dynamic games with incomplete information. In the next section, we will explore the properties of BNE and how it differs from a traditional Nash equilibrium.




### Subsection: 4.1c Applications in Game Theory

In this section, we will explore some applications of Bayesian Nash equilibrium (BNE) in game theory. These applications will help us understand the practical implications of BNE and how it can be used to analyze real-world scenarios.

#### Example 1: The Prisoner's Dilemma

The Prisoner's Dilemma is a classic game in game theory that illustrates the concept of BNE. In this game, two prisoners are interrogated separately and each has the option to confess or remain silent. If both remain silent, they each serve a short sentence. If both confess, they each serve a longer sentence. If one confesses and the other remains silent, the confessor goes free while the other serves a longer sentence.

The payoff matrix of this game for both players is as follows:

| Player 1 | Player 2 |
|---------|---------|
| Confess | Confess | 4 years | 10 years |
| Confess | Remain Silent | 0 years | 8 years |
| Remain Silent | Confess | 8 years | 0 years |
| Remain Silent | Remain Silent | 2 years | 2 years |

In this game, the strategy profile (confess, confess) is a Bayesian Nash equilibrium. This is because if both players are rational and both know that both players are rational and everything that is known by any player is known to be known by every player (i.e. player 1 knows player 2 knows that player 1 is rational), then the strategy profile (confess, confess) is the only strategy profile that is a BNE. This is because if both players confess, they each serve a shorter sentence than if they both remain silent, and if one confesses and the other remains silent, the confessor goes free while the other serves a longer sentence.

#### Example 2: The Sheriff's Dilemma

As discussed in the previous section, the Sheriff's Dilemma is another example of a game where BNE can be applied. In this game, a sheriff faces an armed suspect. Both must simultaneously decide whether to shoot the other or not. The suspect can either be of type "criminal" or type "civilian". The sheriff has only one type. The suspect knows its type and the Sheriff's type, but the Sheriff does not know the suspect's type.

The payoff matrix of this game for both players is as follows:

| Player 1 | Player 2 |
|---------|---------|
| Shoot | Shoot | 0 years | 10 years |
| Shoot | Remain Silent | 8 years | 0 years |
| Remain Silent | Shoot | 8 years | 0 years |
| Remain Silent | Remain Silent | 2 years | 2 years |

In this game, the strategy profile (shoot, shoot) is a Bayesian Nash equilibrium. This is because if both players are rational and both know that both players are rational and everything that is known by any player is known to be known by every player (i.e. player 1 knows player 2 knows that player 1 is rational), then the strategy profile (shoot, shoot) is the only strategy profile that is a BNE. This is because if both players shoot, they each serve a shorter sentence than if they both remain silent, and if one shoots and the other remains silent, the shooter goes free while the other serves a longer sentence.




### Subsection: 4.2a Definition of Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium

In the previous section, we discussed the concept of Bayesian Nash equilibrium (BNE) and its applications in game theory. In this section, we will delve deeper into the concept of Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium (PBE), which is a refinement of BNE.

#### 4.2a Definition of Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium

A Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium (PBE) is a solution concept in game theory that describes the behavior of rational players in dynamic games with incomplete information. It is a refinement of Bayesian Nash equilibrium (BNE) and is used to solve games where players take turns but are unsure of the "type" of their opponent.

In a PBE, each player's strategy and beliefs must satisfy the following conditions:

1. The strategies and beliefs must be consistent with the player's type.
2. The strategies and beliefs must be consistent with the player's beliefs about their opponent's type.
3. The strategies and beliefs must be consistent with the player's beliefs about their opponent's beliefs about their type.
4. The strategies and beliefs must be consistent with the player's beliefs about their opponent's beliefs about their opponent's type.
5. And so on, for all possible levels of belief and knowledge.

A PBE is always a Nash equilibrium. This means that no player can improve their payoff by unilaterally changing their strategy.

#### 4.2a Examples of Perfect Bayesian Equilibria

To better understand the concept of PBE, let's consider some examples.

##### Example 1: Gift Game 1

Consider the following game:

| Player 1 | Player 2 |
|---------|---------|
| Give | Not Give |
| Friend | Not Friend |
| Payoff | Payoff |
| 0 | 0 |
| -1 | 2x - 1 |

For any value of $p$, Equilibrium 1 exists, a pooling equilibrium in which both types of sender choose the same action. The sender prefers the payoff of 0 from not giving to the payoff of -1 from sending and not being accepted. Thus, "Give" has zero probability in equilibrium and Bayes's Rule does not restrict the belief "Prob(Friend|Give)" at all. That belief must be pessimistic enough that the receiver prefers the payoff of 0 from rejecting a gift to the expected payoff of $x (1) + (1-x)(-1) = 2x -1$, from accepting, so the requirement that the receiver's strategy maximize his expected payoff given his beliefs necessitates that "Prob(Friend|Give)"<math> \leq .5.</math> On the other hand, "Prob(Friend|Not give) = p$ is required by Bayes's Rule.

##### Example 2: The Sheriff's Dilemma

In the Sheriff's Dilemma, a sheriff faces an armed suspect. Both must simultaneously decide whether to shoot the other or not. The suspect can either be of type "criminal" or "innocent". The sheriff's strategy is to shoot if the suspect is a criminal and not shoot if the suspect is innocent. The suspect's strategy is to shoot if the sheriff is a coward and not shoot if the sheriff is brave. A PBE in this game is for the sheriff to be brave and for the suspect to be innocent. This is because if the sheriff is brave, the suspect will not shoot, and if the suspect is innocent, the sheriff will not shoot. This is a PBE because it satisfies all the conditions of PBE, as discussed above.

In the next section, we will explore some more examples of PBE and discuss their implications in game theory.





#### 4.2b Examples of Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium

In the previous section, we discussed the concept of Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium (PBE) and its definition. In this section, we will explore some examples of PBE to further understand its applications in game theory.

##### Example 1: Gift Game 1

Consider the following game:

| Player 1 | Player 2 |
|---------|---------|
| Give | Not Give |
| Friend | Not Friend |
| Payoff | Payoff |
| 0 | 0 |
| -1 | 2x - 1 |

For any value of $p$, Equilibrium 1 exists, a pooling equilibrium in which both types of sender choose the same action. The sender prefers the payoff of 0 from not giving to the payoff of -1 from sending and not being accepted. Thus, "Give" has zero probability in equilibrium and Bayes's Rule does not restrict the belief "Prob(Friend|Give)" at all. That belief must be pessimistic enough that the receiver prefers the payoff of 0 from rejecting a gift to the expected payoff of $x (1) + (1-x)(-1) = 2x -1$, from accepting, so the requirement that the receiver's strategy maximize his expected payoff given his beliefs necessitates that "Prob(Friend|Give)"<math> \leq .5.</math> On the other hand, "Prob(Friend|Not give) = p$ is required by Bayes's Rule, since both types take that action and it is uninformative about the sender's type.

If $p\geq 1/2$, a second pooling equilibrium exists as well as Equilibrium 1, based on different beliefs:

The sender prefers the payoff of 1 from giving to the payoff of 0 from not giving, expecting that his gift will be accepted. In equilibrium, Bayes's Rule requires the receiver to have the belief "Prob(Friend|Give) = p", since both types take that action and it is uninformative about the sender's type in this equilibrium. The out-of-equilibrium belief does not matter, since the sender would not want to deviate to "Not give" no matter what response the receiver would have.

Equilibrium 1 is perverse if $p \geq .5$. The game could have $p=.99$, so the sender is very likely a friend, but the receiver still would refuse any gift because he thinks enemies are much more likely than friends to give gifts. This shows how pessimistic beliefs can result in an equilibrium bad for both players, one that is not Pareto efficient. These beliefs seem unrealistic, though, and are often not observed in real-world scenarios.

##### Example 2: Gift Game 2

Consider the following game:

| Player 1 | Player 2 |
|---------|---------|
| Give | Not Give |
| Friend | Not Friend |
| Payoff | Payoff |
| 0 | 0 |
| -1 | 2x - 1 |

For any value of $p$, Equilibrium 1 exists, a pooling equilibrium in which both types of sender choose the same action. The sender prefers the payoff of 0 from not giving to the payoff of -1 from sending and not being accepted. Thus, "Give" has zero probability in equilibrium and Bayes's Rule does not restrict the belief "Prob(Friend|Give)" at all. That belief must be pessimistic enough that the receiver prefers the payoff of 0 from rejecting a gift to the expected payoff of $x (1) + (1-x)(-1) = 2x -1$, from accepting, so the requirement that the receiver's strategy maximize his expected payoff given his beliefs necessitates that "Prob(Friend|Give)"<math> \leq .5.</math> On the other hand, "Prob(Friend|Not give) = p$ is required by Bayes's Rule, since both types take that action and it is uninformative about the sender's type.

If $p\geq 1/2$, a second pooling equilibrium exists as well as Equilibrium 1, based on different beliefs:

The sender prefers the payoff of 1 from giving to the payoff of 0 from not giving, expecting that his gift will be accepted. In equilibrium, Bayes's Rule requires the receiver to have the belief "Prob(Friend|Give) = p", since both types take that action and it is uninformative about the sender's type in this equilibrium. The out-of-equilibrium belief does not matter, since the sender would not want to deviate to "Not give" no matter what response the receiver would have.

Equilibrium 1 is perverse if $p \geq .5$. The game could have $p=.99$, so the sender is very likely a friend, but the receiver still would refuse any gift because he thinks enemies are much more likely than friends to give gifts. This shows how pessimistic beliefs can result in an equilibrium bad for both players, one that is not Pareto efficient. These beliefs seem unrealistic, though, and are often not observed in real-world scenarios.

##### Example 3: Gift Game 3

Consider the following game:

| Player 1 | Player 2 |
|---------|---------|
| Give | Not Give |
| Friend | Not Friend |
| Payoff | Payoff |
| 0 | 0 |
| -1 | 2x - 1 |

For any value of $p$, Equilibrium 1 exists, a pooling equilibrium in which both types of sender choose the same action. The sender prefers the payoff of 0 from not giving to the payoff of -1 from sending and not being accepted. Thus, "Give" has zero probability in equilibrium and Bayes's Rule does not restrict the belief "Prob(Friend|Give)" at all. These beliefs seem unrealistic, though, and are often not observed in real-world scenarios.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the concept of Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium (PBE) in dynamic games with incomplete information. We have seen how PBE is a powerful tool for analyzing strategic interactions between rational agents, and how it can be used to predict the outcomes of games. We have also discussed the limitations and assumptions of PBE, and how it may not always accurately represent real-world scenarios.

We have learned that PBE is a refinement of the concept of Bayesian Nash equilibrium (BNE), and that it requires agents to have consistent beliefs about the types of their opponents. This consistency is crucial for the equilibrium to hold, and any deviation from it can lead to the breakdown of the equilibrium.

Furthermore, we have seen how PBE can be applied to a variety of games, including those with multiple players and those with sequential decision-making. We have also discussed the role of information in PBE, and how it can affect the outcomes of games.

In conclusion, Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium is a valuable tool for understanding strategic interactions between rational agents. However, it is important to remember its limitations and to use it appropriately in the context of the specific game being analyzed.

## Chapter: Chapter 5: Dynamic Games with Complete Information

### Introduction

In the realm of game theory, dynamic games with complete information hold a significant place. These games are characterized by the fact that all players have complete information about the game, including the strategies and payoffs of all other players. This chapter, "Dynamic Games with Complete Information," will delve into the intricacies of these games, exploring their unique characteristics and the strategies that players employ to maximize their payoffs.

Dynamic games with complete information are a type of game where players make decisions sequentially, with each player aware of the decisions made by the players who have played before them. This awareness of the strategies and payoffs of other players can significantly influence the decisions made by each player. The strategies employed in these games are often complex and strategic, as players must consider not only their own payoffs but also the potential reactions of their opponents.

In this chapter, we will explore the mathematical models used to analyze these games, including the concepts of Nash equilibrium and subgame perfect equilibrium. We will also discuss the role of information in these games, and how it can be used to advantage or disadvantage players.

The study of dynamic games with complete information is crucial in many fields, including economics, political science, and biology. Understanding these games can provide insights into strategic decision-making, competition, and cooperation among rational agents.

As we journey through this chapter, we will use the powerful language of mathematics to describe and analyze these games. We will employ concepts such as payoff matrices, best-response functions, and the concept of a Nash equilibrium. We will also use the popular Markdown format to present our content, making it easy to read and understand.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of dynamic games with complete information, and be equipped with the tools to analyze and predict the outcomes of these games. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or simply a curious mind, we hope that this chapter will provide you with a deeper understanding of these fascinating games.




#### 4.2c Application in Game Theory

In the previous section, we discussed the concept of Perfect Bayesian Equilibrium (PBE) and its definition. We also explored some examples of PBE to further understand its applications in game theory. In this section, we will delve deeper into the application of PBE in game theory, specifically in the context of dynamic games with incomplete information.

##### Example 2: Gift Game 2

Consider the following game:

| Player 1 | Player 2 |
|---------|---------|
| Give | Not Give |
| Friend | Not Friend |
| Payoff | Payoff |
| 0 | 0 |
| -1 | 2x - 1 |

For any value of $p$, Equilibrium 1 exists, a pooling equilibrium in which both types of sender choose the same action. The sender prefers the payoff of 0 from not giving to the payoff of -1 from sending and not being accepted. Thus, "Give" has zero probability in equilibrium and Bayes's Rule does not restrict the belief "Prob(Friend|Give)" at all. That belief must be pessimistic enough that the receiver prefers the payoff of 0 from rejecting a gift to the expected payoff of $x (1) + (1-x)(-1) = 2x -1$, from accepting, so the requirement that the receiver's strategy maximize his expected payoff given his beliefs necessitates that "Prob(Friend|Give)"<math> \leq .5.</math> On the other hand, "Prob(Friend|Not give) = p$ is required by Bayes's Rule, since both types take that action and it is uninformative about the sender's type.

If $p\geq 1/2$, a second pooling equilibrium exists as well as Equilibrium 1, based on different beliefs:

The sender prefers the payoff of 1 from giving to the payoff of 0 from not giving, expecting that his gift will be accepted. In equilibrium, Bayes's Rule requires the receiver to have the belief "Prob(Friend|Give) = p", since both types take that action and it is uninformative about the sender's type in this equilibrium. The out-of-equilibrium belief does not matter, since the sender would not want to deviate to "Not give" no matter what response the receiver would have.

Equilibrium 1 is perverse if $p \geq .5$. The game could have $p=.99$, so the sender is very likely a friend, but the receiver still prefers to reject the gift. This is because the receiver's payoff from accepting is only $2x - 1$, while the payoff from rejecting is 0. This example highlights the importance of understanding the beliefs and payoffs of each player in a game, as they can greatly influence the outcome of the game.




#### 4.3a Definition of Signaling Games

In the realm of game theory, signaling games are a type of dynamic Bayesian game that are characterized by one player taking an action, known as a signal, to convey information to another player. This action is often more costly if the player is conveying false information. The essence of a signaling game lies in the fact that the sender, or the player taking the action, has one of two types, one desirable and one undesirable, with different payoff functions. The receiver, on the other hand, has just one type and knows the probability of each type, but not which one the sender has.

The signaling game is a simple yet powerful tool in game theory, with its applications extending to various fields such as economics, political science, and biology. It is often used to model situations where one player has private information about their type and needs to signal this information to another player. The receiver, in turn, needs to interpret this signal to make a decision that maximizes their payoff.

The classic example of a signaling game is that of a worker who acquires a college degree not because it increases their skill, but because it conveys their ability to employers. The worker's decision to acquire a degree is a signal to the employer about their ability, and the employer's decision to hire the worker is based on their interpretation of this signal.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the concept of signaling games, exploring their properties, equilibria, and applications. We will also discuss the Lewis signaling game, a specific type of signaling game that features perfect common interest between players.

#### 4.3b Properties of Signaling Games

Signaling games, due to their inherent complexity and strategic nature, exhibit several interesting properties. These properties are often crucial in understanding the dynamics of the game and predicting the outcomes. In this section, we will explore some of these properties.

##### 1. Informativeness

One of the key properties of signaling games is informativeness. This refers to the ability of the signal to convey information about the sender's type to the receiver. In a signaling game, the sender's type is often private information, and the signal is the only means for the sender to communicate this information to the receiver. The informativeness of the signal is determined by the cost of sending the signal. If the cost of sending the signal is high, the sender is less likely to send a false signal, making the signal more informative.

##### 2. Credibility

Another important property of signaling games is credibility. This refers to the receiver's belief in the signal. If the receiver believes that the signal is credible, they are more likely to act on it. The credibility of the signal is influenced by the sender's reputation and the receiver's prior beliefs about the sender's type.

##### 3. Equilibrium

Signaling games often have multiple equilibria. An equilibrium is a set of strategies where no player can improve their payoff by unilaterally changing their strategy. In signaling games, these equilibria often involve a combination of signaling and non-signaling strategies. For example, in the classic signaling game of a worker signaling their ability to an employer, the equilibrium might involve the worker signaling their ability (e.g., by acquiring a college degree) and the employer hiring the worker based on this signal.

##### 4. Common Interest

In some signaling games, there is a common interest between the sender and the receiver. This means that both players want the same outcome, and the signal is used to coordinate this outcome. The Lewis signaling game, named after philosopher David Lewis, is an example of such a game. In the Lewis signaling game, both players have the same payoff function, and the signal is used to coordinate their actions.

In the next section, we will explore some specific examples of signaling games to further illustrate these properties.

#### 4.3c Application in Game Theory

Signaling games have a wide range of applications in game theory. They are used to model situations where one player has private information about their type and needs to signal this information to another player. This section will explore some of these applications, focusing on their relevance in economics and political science.

##### 4.3c.1 Market Signaling

One of the most common applications of signaling games is in market signaling. In this context, the sender is a seller in a market, and the receiver is a buyer. The seller has private information about the quality of the good they are selling, and they need to signal this information to the buyer. The buyer, on the other hand, needs to interpret this signal to make a decision about whether to buy the good.

For example, consider a market for used cars. The seller knows the condition of the car, which can range from excellent to poor. The buyer, however, does not have this information. The seller can signal the condition of the car by setting a price. A higher price can signal a better condition, but it also involves a higher cost for the seller. The buyer, in turn, needs to interpret this signal to decide whether to buy the car.

##### 4.3c.2 Political Signaling

Signaling games also have applications in political science. In this context, the sender can be a politician, and the receiver can be a voter. The politician has private information about their policy preferences, and they need to signal this information to the voter. The voter, on the other hand, needs to interpret this signal to decide how to vote.

For example, consider a politician who is running for office. The politician has private information about their policy preferences, which can range from liberal to conservative. The voter, however, does not have this information. The politician can signal their policy preferences by taking a public stance on certain issues. A more liberal stance can signal more liberal policy preferences, but it also involves a higher cost for the politician. The voter, in turn, needs to interpret this signal to decide how to vote.

##### 4.3c.3 Other Applications

Signaling games have many other applications in economics and political science. They are used to model situations where one player has private information about their type and needs to signal this information to another player. This includes situations such as job interviews, negotiations, and auctions.

In the next section, we will explore some specific examples of signaling games to further illustrate these applications.

In this chapter, we have delved into the complex world of dynamic games with incomplete information. We have explored the fundamental concepts, strategies, and outcomes of these games, and how they differ from static games. We have also examined the role of information in these games, and how it can be used to strategically advantage one player over another.

We have learned that dynamic games with incomplete information are characterized by the fact that players make decisions sequentially, and that each player only has partial information about the game. This makes these games inherently more complex and strategic than static games, where all players have complete information about the game.

We have also seen how these games can be modeled using game theory, and how the outcomes of these games can be predicted using concepts such as Nash equilibrium and subgame perfect equilibrium. These concepts provide a powerful tool for understanding and predicting the behavior of players in these games.

In conclusion, dynamic games with incomplete information are a rich and complex area of study in microeconomic theory. They provide a powerful framework for understanding strategic decision-making in a wide range of economic and social contexts.

## Chapter: Chapter 5: Dynamic Games with Complete Information:

### Introduction

In the realm of microeconomic theory, the study of dynamic games with complete information is a crucial aspect. This chapter delves into the intricacies of these games, providing a comprehensive understanding of their principles, strategies, and outcomes. 

Dynamic games with complete information are a type of game where all players have complete information about the game, including the strategies and payoffs of all other players. This is in contrast to dynamic games with incomplete information, where players may only have partial information about the game. The complete information in these games allows for a more strategic and complex decision-making process, as players can anticipate and respond to the actions of their opponents.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental concepts of dynamic games with complete information, including the Nash equilibrium, subgame perfect equilibrium, and the role of information in these games. We will also delve into the mathematical models used to represent these games, such as the extensive form and the payoff matrix. 

We will also discuss the applications of these games in various economic scenarios, such as pricing strategies in oligopolies, bargaining games, and auctions. By the end of this chapter, readers should have a solid understanding of the principles and strategies involved in dynamic games with complete information, and be able to apply this knowledge to real-world economic situations.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive guide to advanced game theory and mechanism design, equipping readers with the necessary tools to understand and analyze dynamic games with complete information. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a practitioner in the field of microeconomics, this chapter will serve as a valuable resource in your journey to mastering these complex games.




#### 4.3b Examples of Signaling Games

Signaling games are ubiquitous in various fields, and understanding their properties is crucial for predicting outcomes in these games. In this section, we will explore some examples of signaling games to illustrate the concepts discussed in the previous section.

##### Example 1: The Sir Philip Sidney Game

The Sir Philip Sidney game is a classic example of a signaling game. In this game, there are two individuals, the signaler and the responder. The responder has some good which can be transferred to the signaler or not. If the responder keeps the good, the responder has a fitness of 1, otherwise the responder has a fitness of (1-"d"). The signaler can be in one of two states, "healthy" or "needy". If the signaler receives the good, the signaler has a fitness of 1, otherwise the signaler has a fitness of (1-"c").

The signaler moves first, choosing whether to signal that they are healthy or needy. The responder then moves, choosing whether to transfer the good or not. The payoff for the signaler is the fitness minus the cost of signaling, and the payoff for the responder is the fitness minus the cost of transferring the good.

This game is a signaling game because the signaler has private information about their state and needs to signal this information to the responder. The responder, in turn, needs to interpret this signal to make a decision that maximizes their payoff.

##### Example 2: The Lewis Signaling Game

The Lewis signaling game is another classic example of a signaling game. In this game, there are two players, the sender and the receiver. The sender has one of two types, "good" or "bad", with different payoff functions. The receiver knows the probability of each type but not which one the sender has.

The sender moves first, choosing whether to send a "good" signal or a "bad" signal. The receiver then moves, choosing whether to accept the signal or not. The payoff for the sender is the payoff minus the cost of signaling, and the payoff for the receiver is the payoff minus the cost of accepting the signal.

This game is a signaling game because the sender has private information about their type and needs to signal this information to the receiver. The receiver, in turn, needs to interpret this signal to make a decision that maximizes their payoff.

These examples illustrate the key features of signaling games: private information, strategic signaling, and interpretation of signals. Understanding these features is crucial for analyzing the outcomes of signaling games. In the next section, we will explore the concept of equilibria in signaling games.

#### Example 3: The Worker-Firm Signaling Game

The Worker-Firm Signaling Game is a classic example of a signaling game. In this game, there are two players, a worker and a firm. The worker has one of two types, "good" or "bad", with different payoff functions. The firm knows the probability of each type but not which one the worker has.

The worker moves first, choosing whether to exert effort or not. The firm then moves, choosing whether to hire the worker or not. The payoff for the worker is the payoff minus the cost of exerting effort, and the payoff for the firm is the payoff minus the cost of hiring the worker.

This game is a signaling game because the worker has private information about their type and needs to signal this information to the firm. The firm, in turn, needs to interpret this signal to make a decision that maximizes their payoff.

#### Example 4: The Dating Game

The Dating Game is another classic example of a signaling game. In this game, there are two players, a man and a woman. The man has one of two types, "rich" or "poor", with different payoff functions. The woman knows the probability of each type but not which one the man has.

The man moves first, choosing whether to buy a gift or not. The woman then moves, choosing whether to go on a date or not. The payoff for the man is the payoff minus the cost of buying the gift, and the payoff for the woman is the payoff minus the cost of going on the date.

This game is a signaling game because the man has private information about their type and needs to signal this information to the woman. The woman, in turn, needs to interpret this signal to make a decision that maximizes their payoff.

These examples illustrate the key features of signaling games: private information, strategic signaling, and interpretation of signals. Understanding these features is crucial for predicting outcomes in signaling games.




#### 4.3c Application in Game Theory

Signaling games have a wide range of applications in game theory. They are used to model situations where one player has private information about their type and needs to signal this information to another player. This can occur in various scenarios, such as job markets, dating, and political elections.

##### Job Market Signaling

In the job market, signaling games can be used to model the interaction between a job seeker and a potential employer. The job seeker has private information about their skills and abilities, which they need to signal to the employer. The employer, on the other hand, knows the probability of each type of job seeker but not which one the job seeker is.

The job seeker moves first, choosing whether to signal that they are skilled or unskilled. The employer then moves, choosing whether to hire the job seeker or not. The payoff for the job seeker is the wage minus the cost of signaling, and the payoff for the employer is the productivity of the job seeker minus the cost of hiring.

##### Dating Signaling

In dating, signaling games can be used to model the interaction between two individuals. Each individual has private information about their attractiveness and needs to signal this information to the other. The other individual, in turn, needs to interpret this signal to make a decision about whether to pursue a relationship.

The individual moves first, choosing whether to signal that they are attractive or unattractive. The other individual then moves, choosing whether to pursue a relationship or not. The payoff for the individual is the utility of the relationship minus the cost of signaling, and the payoff for the other individual is the utility of the relationship minus the cost of pursuing it.

##### Political Elections

In political elections, signaling games can be used to model the interaction between a politician and the electorate. The politician has private information about their policies and needs to signal this information to the electorate. The electorate, on the other hand, knows the probability of each type of politician but not which one the politician is.

The politician moves first, choosing whether to signal that they are liberal or conservative. The electorate then moves, choosing whether to vote for the politician or not. The payoff for the politician is the utility of being elected minus the cost of signaling, and the payoff for the electorate is the utility of the politician's policies minus the cost of voting.

In all these applications, the key is to understand the private information of the players, the signals they can send, and the decisions they need to make. By modeling these situations as signaling games, we can gain insights into the strategic interactions between the players and predict the outcomes of these interactions.




#### 4.4a Definition of Screening Games

Screening games are a type of dynamic game with incomplete information that are used in economic and game theoretical modeling. They are closely related to signaling games, but there is a key difference in how information is exchanged. In screening games, the player who has private information about their type (the agent) makes a decision about how much information to reveal to the other player (the principal) based on the principal's offer. This is in contrast to signaling games, where the agent makes a decision about how much information to reveal before the principal makes an offer.

The principal-agent model is a common framework for understanding screening games. In this model, there is an employer (the principal) and a worker (the agent). The worker has a given skill level, and chooses the amount of effort he will exert. If the worker knows his ability (which is given at the outset, perhaps by nature), and can acquire credentials or somehow signal that ability to the employer before being offered a wage, then the problem is signaling. However, in screening games, the employer offers a wage level first, at which point the worker chooses the amount of credentials he will acquire (perhaps in the form of education or skills) and accepts or rejects a contract for a wage level. This is called screening, because the worker is screened by the employer in that the offers may be contingent on the skill level of the worker.

Some economists use the terms signaling and screening interchangeably, and the distinction can be attributed to Stiglitz and Weiss (1989). However, it is important to note that while signaling games and screening games are similar, they are not the same. In signaling games, the agent makes a decision about how much information to reveal before the principal makes an offer. In screening games, the agent makes a decision about how much information to reveal after the principal makes an offer. This distinction is crucial in understanding the strategic interactions between the principal and agent in these games.

In the next section, we will explore the different types of screening games and their applications in various economic scenarios.

#### 4.4b Characteristics of Screening Games

Screening games, as we have seen, are a type of dynamic game with incomplete information. They are characterized by several key features that distinguish them from other types of games. These features are largely derived from the nature of the principal-agent relationship and the strategic interactions that occur within this relationship.

##### Asymmetry of Information

One of the defining characteristics of screening games is the asymmetry of information between the principal and the agent. The agent, in this case, the worker, has private information about their skill level, while the principal, the employer, does not. This asymmetry of information creates a strategic interaction between the two players, as the agent must decide how much information to reveal to the principal based on the principal's offer.

##### Contingent Contracts

In screening games, the principal offers a wage level first, at which point the agent chooses the amount of credentials he will acquire and accepts or rejects a contract for a wage level. This is a key feature of screening games and sets them apart from signaling games. The principal's offer is contingent on the agent's skill level, and the agent's decision to accept or reject the offer is based on this contingency.

##### Signaling and Screening

While some economists use the terms signaling and screening interchangeably, it is important to note that there is a distinction between the two. In signaling games, the agent makes a decision about how much information to reveal before the principal makes an offer. In screening games, the agent makes a decision about how much information to reveal after the principal makes an offer. This distinction is crucial in understanding the strategic interactions between the principal and agent in these games.

##### Strategic Interactions

The strategic interactions between the principal and agent are a key feature of screening games. The agent must decide how much information to reveal based on the principal's offer, and the principal must decide how much to offer based on the agent's potential skill level. This creates a dynamic game with incomplete information, where the players' decisions are influenced by their beliefs about the other player's type and behavior.

In the next section, we will explore the different types of screening games and their applications in various economic scenarios.

#### 4.4c Applications of Screening Games

Screening games have a wide range of applications in various economic scenarios. They are particularly useful in situations where there is asymmetry of information between two parties, and where the actions of one party are influenced by the actions of the other. In this section, we will explore some of these applications in more detail.

##### Employment Contracts

One of the most common applications of screening games is in the context of employment contracts. As we have seen in the previous sections, the principal-agent model is a key framework for understanding screening games. In this model, the principal (employer) and the agent (worker) have different information about the worker's skill level. The worker knows their skill level, while the employer does not. The worker must decide how much information to reveal to the employer based on the employer's offer. This is a classic example of a screening game.

The employer, in turn, must decide how much to offer the worker based on their potential skill level. This is a contingent contract, as the offer is contingent on the worker's skill level. The worker's decision to accept or reject the offer is based on this contingency. This creates a dynamic game with incomplete information, where the players' decisions are influenced by their beliefs about the other player's type and behavior.

##### Market Equilibrium Computation

Another important application of screening games is in the computation of market equilibrium. Gao, Peysakhovich, and Kroer recently presented an algorithm for online computation of market equilibrium. This algorithm uses the concept of screening games to compute the equilibrium price and quantity in a market.

The algorithm works by setting up a screening game between the buyers and sellers in the market. The buyers, who have private information about their valuation of the good, must decide how much information to reveal to the sellers based on the sellers' offer. The sellers, in turn, must decide how much to offer the buyers based on their potential valuation. This creates a dynamic game with incomplete information, where the players' decisions are influenced by their beliefs about the other player's type and behavior.

##### Other Applications

Screening games have many other applications in economics and game theory. They are used in auctions, matching markets, and bargaining situations, among others. In each of these applications, the key feature is the asymmetry of information between the players, and the strategic interactions that this asymmetry creates.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the mathematical foundations of screening games, and explore some of the key results and techniques used in their analysis.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the complex world of dynamic games with incomplete information. We have explored the fundamental concepts, strategies, and outcomes of these games, and how they differ from static games. We have also examined the role of information in these games, and how it can be used to advantage or disadvantage players.

We have seen that dynamic games with incomplete information are characterized by a constant flow of information, which can change the game's dynamics and outcomes. This flow of information can be used by players to adjust their strategies and gain an advantage over their opponents. However, it can also lead to uncertainty and risk, as players may not have all the information they need to make optimal decisions.

We have also learned that these games can be solved using various techniques, such as backward induction and subgame perfect equilibrium. These techniques allow us to determine the optimal strategies for each player, and predict the game's outcome.

In conclusion, dynamic games with incomplete information are a rich and complex field of study. They provide a powerful framework for understanding strategic interactions in a wide range of economic and social contexts. By mastering the concepts and techniques presented in this chapter, you will be well-equipped to tackle more advanced topics in game theory and mechanism design.

## Chapter: Chapter 5: Dynamic Games with Complete Information:

### Introduction

In the realm of microeconomic theory, the study of dynamic games with complete information is a crucial aspect that provides a deeper understanding of strategic decision-making over time. This chapter will delve into the intricacies of these games, exploring the fundamental concepts, strategies, and outcomes that govern them.

Dynamic games with complete information are a type of game where all players have complete knowledge of the game, including the strategies and payoffs of all other players. This is in contrast to dynamic games with incomplete information, where players may have private information about their own strategies or payoffs. The complete information setting allows for a more straightforward analysis of the game, as all players have the same information and can therefore make more informed decisions.

The chapter will begin by introducing the basic concepts of dynamic games, including the notions of a strategy, a payoff, and a Nash equilibrium. We will then move on to discuss the specific characteristics of games with complete information, highlighting the role of information in shaping the game's dynamics and outcomes.

We will also explore various solution concepts for these games, such as the subgame perfect equilibrium and the perfect Bayesian equilibrium. These concepts provide a framework for analyzing the strategic interactions between players over time, and for predicting the outcomes of the game.

Finally, we will examine some real-world applications of dynamic games with complete information, demonstrating the practical relevance of these games in various economic contexts.

By the end of this chapter, readers should have a solid understanding of the principles and techniques used to analyze dynamic games with complete information. This knowledge will serve as a foundation for more advanced topics in game theory and mechanism design, and will be invaluable for anyone seeking to understand strategic decision-making in dynamic settings.




#### 4.4b Examples of Screening Games

Screening games are a fundamental concept in game theory and have a wide range of applications in economics. In this section, we will explore some examples of screening games to further illustrate their importance and relevance.

##### Example 1: The Market for Lemons

The market for lemons is a classic example of a screening game. In this game, a seller has a car that is either a "lemon" (of low quality) or a "peach" (of high quality). The seller knows the quality of the car, but the buyer does not. The seller offers the car to the buyer at a price, and the buyer decides whether to buy the car or not. If the buyer buys the car, they learn the quality of the car. If they do not buy the car, they learn nothing.

This game is a screening game because the seller chooses how much information to reveal to the buyer by setting the price. If the seller sets a high price, they are screening out buyers who are not willing to pay a high price, which are likely to be buyers who are willing to accept a low-quality car. Conversely, if the seller sets a low price, they are screening in buyers who are willing to pay a low price, which are likely to be buyers who are willing to accept a high-quality car.

##### Example 2: The Job Market

The job market is another example of a screening game. In this game, a job seeker has a given level of ability, and chooses the amount of effort they will exert in their job search. The job seeker then applies for jobs, and the employer offers a wage level. The job seeker decides whether to accept the job offer or not. If they accept the job offer, they learn the wage level. If they do not accept the job offer, they learn nothing.

This game is a screening game because the job seeker chooses how much information to reveal to the employer by accepting or rejecting the job offer. If the job seeker accepts a high wage offer, they are screening out employers who are not willing to pay a high wage, which are likely to be employers who are willing to accept a low-ability worker. Conversely, if the job seeker accepts a low wage offer, they are screening in employers who are willing to pay a low wage, which are likely to be employers who are willing to accept a high-ability worker.

These examples illustrate the importance of screening games in understanding economic phenomena. By understanding the incentives of the players and the information they have, we can gain insights into how markets and other economic systems operate.

#### 4.4c Solutions to Screening Games

Solving screening games involves understanding the incentives of the players and the information they have. The solution to a screening game is typically a strategy for each player that maximizes their payoff, given the strategies of the other players. In this section, we will explore some solutions to the screening games we have discussed.

##### Solution to the Market for Lemons

The solution to the market for lemons is typically a price-discriminating equilibrium. In this equilibrium, the seller sets a high price for the lemon and a low price for the peach. The buyer learns the quality of the car by buying it, and the seller learns the type of the buyer by the price they are willing to pay. This solution is often referred to as the "winner-takes-all" solution, as the seller wins by selling the car to the buyer who is willing to pay the highest price.

##### Solution to the Job Market

The solution to the job market is typically a wage-discriminating equilibrium. In this equilibrium, the job seeker accepts a high wage offer and rejects a low wage offer. The employer learns the ability of the job seeker by the wage they are willing to accept. This solution is often referred to as the "winner-takes-all" solution, as the job seeker wins by accepting the job offer from the employer who is willing to pay the highest wage.

These solutions illustrate the importance of screening games in understanding economic phenomena. By understanding the incentives of the players and the information they have, we can gain insights into how markets and other economic systems operate.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the complex world of dynamic games with incomplete information. We have explored the fundamental concepts, strategies, and outcomes of these games, and how they differ from static games. We have also examined the role of information in these games, and how it can be used to advantage or disadvantage players.

We have seen that dynamic games with incomplete information are a rich and complex field, with many possible strategies and outcomes. These games are particularly relevant in the realm of economics, where they can help us understand the behavior of firms and individuals in dynamic, uncertain environments.

However, we have also seen that these games are not without their challenges. The presence of incomplete information can make it difficult to predict outcomes, and the dynamic nature of these games can lead to a wide range of possible strategies. Nevertheless, with the tools and concepts we have discussed in this chapter, we are better equipped to navigate these complexities and gain a deeper understanding of these games.

## Chapter: Chapter 5: Dynamic Games with Complete Information:

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 5 of "Microeconomic Theory II: A Comprehensive Guide to Advanced Game Theory and Mechanism Design". In this chapter, we delve into the fascinating world of dynamic games with complete information. This chapter is designed to provide a comprehensive understanding of the advanced concepts of game theory and mechanism design, specifically focusing on dynamic games where all players have complete information about the game.

Dynamic games are a crucial aspect of microeconomic theory, as they allow us to model and analyze situations where decisions are made over time. In these games, players make decisions sequentially, and the decisions of previous players can influence the decisions of subsequent players. This adds a layer of complexity to the game, as players must not only consider their own decisions but also the decisions of others.

In contrast to dynamic games with incomplete information, where players do not have complete information about the game, dynamic games with complete information are a simpler and more tractable class of games. In these games, all players have perfect information about the game, including the decisions of previous players. This simplifies the analysis of the game, as players can make decisions based on the full information available to them.

Throughout this chapter, we will explore the key concepts and techniques used to analyze dynamic games with complete information. We will start by introducing the basic concepts of dynamic games, including the notions of a player's decision sequence and the payoff function. We will then move on to more advanced topics, such as the Nash equilibrium and the subgame perfect equilibrium. We will also discuss the role of mechanism design in these games, and how it can be used to design efficient and incentive-compatible mechanisms.

By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of the theory and applications of dynamic games with complete information. You will be equipped with the knowledge and tools to analyze these games and understand the strategic interactions between players. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a practitioner in the field of economics, this chapter will provide you with a comprehensive guide to advanced game theory and mechanism design in the context of dynamic games with complete information.




#### 4.4c Application in Game Theory

Game theory is a powerful tool for analyzing strategic decision-making in a variety of contexts. Screening games, in particular, have been applied in numerous areas of economics, including labor markets, product markets, and financial markets. In this section, we will explore some of these applications in more detail.

##### Labor Markets

In labor markets, screening games can be used to model the job search process. As mentioned in the previous section, a job seeker chooses the amount of effort they will exert in their job search, and then applies for jobs. The employer offers a wage level, and the job seeker decides whether to accept the job offer or not. This is a screening game because the job seeker chooses how much information to reveal to the employer by accepting or rejecting the job offer.

For example, if the job seeker accepts a high wage offer, they are screening out employers who are not willing to pay a high wage. This can be modeled as a screening game, where the job seeker is the sender and the employer is the receiver. The job seeker chooses how much information to reveal to the employer by accepting or rejecting the job offer. The employer then makes a decision based on this information.

##### Product Markets

In product markets, screening games can be used to model the behavior of firms. Firms often have private information about the quality of their products, and they choose how much to reveal to consumers by setting the price of their product. Consumers then decide whether to buy the product or not, and learn the quality of the product if they do.

For example, consider a market for used cars. Each car has a private quality level, and the seller knows this quality level. The seller sets a price for the car, and the buyer decides whether to buy the car or not. If the buyer buys the car, they learn the quality of the car. This is a screening game, where the seller is the sender and the buyer is the receiver. The seller chooses how much information to reveal to the buyer by setting the price of the car. The buyer then makes a decision based on this information.

##### Financial Markets

In financial markets, screening games can be used to model the behavior of investors. Investors often have private information about the quality of a stock, and they choose how much to reveal to other investors by buying or selling the stock. Other investors then decide whether to buy or sell the stock, and learn the quality of the stock if they do.

For example, consider a market for a stock with a private quality level. Each investor has a private belief about the quality of the stock, and they choose whether to buy or sell the stock. The price of the stock is determined by the market, and investors learn the quality of the stock if they buy the stock. This is a screening game, where each investor is both a sender and a receiver. They choose how much information to reveal to other investors by buying or selling the stock, and they make decisions based on the information revealed by other investors.

In conclusion, screening games are a powerful tool for analyzing strategic decision-making in a variety of contexts. They allow us to model situations where one player has private information and chooses how much to reveal to another player, who then makes a decision based on this information. By studying these games, we can gain insights into the behavior of firms, investors, and job seekers in various markets.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the complex world of dynamic games with incomplete information. We have explored the fundamental concepts, strategies, and outcomes of these games, and how they differ from static games. We have also examined the role of information in these games, and how it can be used to advantage or disadvantage players.

We have learned that dynamic games with incomplete information are characterized by the fact that players make decisions sequentially, and that each player only has partial information about the game. This adds a layer of complexity to the game, as players must not only consider their own decisions, but also the decisions of other players and how they might react to those decisions.

We have also seen how these games can be modeled and analyzed using various techniques, such as backward induction and subgame perfect equilibrium. These techniques allow us to predict the outcomes of these games, and to understand the strategies that players might employ.

In conclusion, dynamic games with incomplete information are a rich and complex field of study. They provide a powerful framework for understanding strategic decision-making in a wide range of contexts, from economics and politics to biology and psychology. By mastering the concepts and techniques presented in this chapter, you will be well-equipped to tackle more advanced topics in game theory and mechanism design.

In this chapter, we have explored the fascinating world of dynamic games with incomplete information. We have seen how these games differ from static games and how they can be used to model real-world situations where players make decisions over time and do not have complete information about the game. We have also learned about the different types of incomplete information, including private information, public information, and common information, and how they can affect the outcome of a game.

One of the key takeaways from this chapter is the importance of understanding the dynamics of a game. In dynamic games, players not only have to make decisions at a given point in time, but they also have to consider how their decisions will affect future decisions and the overall outcome of the game. This requires a deep understanding of the game and its underlying mechanisms, as well as the ability to think strategically and anticipate the actions of other players.

Another important concept we have explored is the role of information in a game. Incomplete information can greatly impact the outcome of a game, as players may have different information about the game and may make decisions based on this information. This can lead to suboptimal outcomes and the need for mechanisms to coordinate players and ensure efficient outcomes.

Overall, this chapter has provided a comprehensive guide to advanced game theory and mechanism design in the context of dynamic games with incomplete information. By understanding the dynamics of a game and the role of information, we can better analyze and design mechanisms to achieve efficient outcomes in a wide range of real-world situations.
